//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG/480px-InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Infarction" title="Infarction">Infarction</a> of the lung due to a pulmonary embolism</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary embolism</a> is a blood clot that becomes lodged in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_arteries" title="Pulmonary arteries">pulmonary arteries</a>. The majority of emboli arise because of <a href="/wiki/Deep_vein_thrombosis" title="Deep vein thrombosis">deep vein thrombosis</a> in the legs. Pulmonary emboli may be investigated using a <a href="/wiki/Ventilation/perfusion_scan" title="Ventilation/perfusion scan">ventilation/perfusion scan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CT_pulmonary_angiography" title="CT pulmonary angiography">a CT scan of the arteries of the lung</a>, or blood tests such as the <a href="/wiki/D-dimer" title="D-dimer">D-dimer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_48-7"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_hypertension" title="Pulmonary hypertension">Pulmonary hypertension</a> describes an increased pressure at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a> that has a large number of differing causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_48-8"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-48">[48]</a></sup> Other rarer conditions may also affect the blood supply of the lung, such as <a href="/wiki/Granulomatosis_with_polyangiitis" title="Granulomatosis with polyangiitis">granulomatosis with polyangiitis</a>, which causes inflammation of the small blood vessels of the lungs and kidneys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_48-9"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_contusion" title="Pulmonary contusion">lung contusion</a> is a bruise caused by chest trauma. It results in hemorrhage of the alveoli causing a build-up of fluid which can impair breathing, and this can be either mild or severe.\nThe function of the lungs can also be affected by compression from fluid in the pleural cavity <a href="/wiki/Pleural_effusion" title="Pleural effusion">pleural effusion</a>, or other substances such as air (<a href="/wiki/Pneumothorax" title="Pneumothorax">pneumothorax</a>), blood (<a href="/wiki/Hemothorax" title="Hemothorax">hemothorax</a>), or rarer causes. These may be investigated using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest_X-ray" title="Chest X-ray">chest X-ray</a> or <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a>, and may require the insertion of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_drain" title="Surgical drain">surgical drain</a> until the underlying cause is identified and treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_48-10"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="528" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg/240px-Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg/360px-Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg/480px-Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lung tissue affected by emphysema using <a href="/wiki/H%26E_stain" title="H&amp;E stain">H&amp;E stain</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">Asthma</a>, chronic <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchiectasis" title="Bronchiectasis">bronchiectasis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a> (COPD) are all <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_lung_disease" title="Obstructive lung disease">obstructive lung diseases</a> characterised by <a href="/wiki/Airway_obstruction" title="Airway obstruction">airway obstruction</a>. This limits the amount of air that is able to enter alveoli because of constriction of the bronchial tree, due to inflammation. Obstructive lung diseases are often identified because of symptoms and diagnosed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_function_tests" title="Pulmonary function tests">pulmonary function tests</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Spirometry" title="Spirometry">spirometry</a>. Many obstructive lung diseases are managed by avoiding triggers (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dust_mite" title="Dust mite">dust mites</a> or <a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">smoking</a>), with symptom control such as <a href="/wiki/Bronchodilator" title="Bronchodilator">bronchodilators</a>, and with suppression of inflammation (such as through <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">corticosteroids</a>) in severe cases. One common cause of COPD and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emphysema" title="Emphysema">emphysema</a> is smoking, and common causes of <a href="/wiki/Bronchiectasis" title="Bronchiectasis">bronchiectasis</a> include severe infections and <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>. The definitive cause of <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> is not yet known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_48-11"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some types of chronic lung diseases are classified as <a href="/wiki/Restrictive_lung_disease" title="Restrictive lung disease">restrictive lung disease</a>, because of a restriction in the amount of lung tissue involved in respiration. These include <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_fibrosis" title="Pulmonary fibrosis">pulmonary fibrosis</a> which can occur when the lung is inflamed for a long period of time. <a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">Fibrosis</a> in the lung replaces functioning lung tissue with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibrous_connective_tissue" title="Fibrous connective tissue">fibrous connective tissue</a>. This can be due to a large variety of <a href="/wiki/Occupational_disease" title="Occupational disease">occupational diseases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Coalworker%27s_pneumoconiosis" title="Coalworker\'s pneumoconiosis">Coalworker\'s pneumoconiosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_disease" title="Autoimmune disease">autoimmune diseases</a> or more rarely to a reaction to <a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">medication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_48-12"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">Lung cancer</a> can either arise directly from lung tissue or as a result of <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a> from another part of the body. There are two main types of primary tumour described as either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small-cell_lung_carcinoma" title="Small-cell lung carcinoma">small-cell</a> or <a href="/wiki/Non-small-cell_lung_carcinoma" title="Non-small-cell lung carcinoma">non-small-cell lung carcinomas</a>. The major risk factor for cancer is <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoking</a>. Once a cancer is identified it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumour_staging" title="Tumour staging">staged</a> using scans such as a <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a> and a sample of tissue (a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/biopsy" title="wikt:biopsy">biopsy</a>) is taken. Cancers may be treated by surgically removing the tumour, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotherapy" title="Radiotherapy">radiotherapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> or combinations thereof, or with the aim of <a href="/wiki/Palliative_care" title="Palliative care">symptom control</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_48-13"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-48">[48]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer_screening" title="Lung cancer screening">Lung cancer screening</a> is being recommended in the United States for high-risk populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital_disorder" title="Congenital disorder">Congenital disorders</a> include <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_hypoplasia" title="Pulmonary hypoplasia">pulmonary hypoplasia</a> (an incomplete development of the lungs)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cadichon_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cadichon-64">[64]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Congenital_diaphragmatic_hernia" title="Congenital diaphragmatic hernia">congenital diaphragmatic hernia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infant_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Infant respiratory distress syndrome">infant respiratory distress syndrome</a> caused by a deficiency in lung surfactant. An <a href="/wiki/Azygos_lobe" title="Azygos lobe">azygos lobe</a> is a congenital <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomical_variation" title="Anatomical variation">anatomical variation</a> which though usually without effect can cause problems in <a href="/wiki/Thoracoscopy" title="Thoracoscopy">thoracoscopic</a> procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Pneumothorax" title="Pneumothorax">pneumothorax</a> (collapsed lung) is an abnormal collection of air in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleural_space" title="Pleural space">pleural space</a> that causes an uncoupling of the lung from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest_wall" title="Chest wall">chest wall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2014_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2014-66">[66]</a></sup> The lung cannot expand against the air pressure inside the pleural space. An easy to understand example is a traumatic pneumothorax, where air enters the pleural space from outside the body, as occurs with puncture to the chest wall. Similarly, a scuba diver ascending while holding their breath with their lungs fully inflated can cause air sacs (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveoli" title="Alveoli">alveoli</a>) to burst and leak high pressure air into the pleural space.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lung_function_testing">Lung function testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Lung function testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_function_testing" title="Pulmonary function testing">Pulmonary function testing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lung_volumes" title="Lung volumes">Lung volumes</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:592px;max-width:592px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LungVolume.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="323" data-file-width="935" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/LungVolume.jpg/400px-LungVolume.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/LungVolume.jpg/600px-LungVolume.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/LungVolume.jpg/800px-LungVolume.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Lung_volumes" title="Lung volumes">Lung volumes</a> as described in the text.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:186px;max-width:186px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DoingSpirometry.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1092" data-file-width="1508" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DoingSpirometry.JPG/184px-DoingSpirometry.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DoingSpirometry.JPG/276px-DoingSpirometry.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DoingSpirometry.JPG/368px-DoingSpirometry.JPG 2x" width="184"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A person doing a <a href="/wiki/Spirometry" title="Spirometry">spirometry</a> test.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_function_testing" title="Pulmonary function testing">Lung function testing</a> is carried out by evaluating a person\'s capacity to inhale and exhale in different circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong2012_34_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong2012_34-67">[67]</a></sup> The volume of air inhaled and exhaled by a person at rest is the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_volume" title="Tidal volume">tidal volume</a> (normally 500-750mL); the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inspiratory_reserve_volume" title="Inspiratory reserve volume">inspiratory reserve volume</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expiratory_reserve_volume" title="Expiratory reserve volume">expiratory reserve volume</a> are the additional amounts a person is able to forcibly inhale and exhale respectively. The summed total of forced inspiration and expiration is a person\'s <a href="/wiki/Vital_capacity" title="Vital capacity">vital capacity</a>. Not all air is expelled from the lungs even after a forced breath out; the remainder of the air is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Residual_volume" title="Residual volume">residual volume</a>. Together these terms are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Lung_volumes" title="Lung volumes">lung volumes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong2012_34_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong2012_34-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pulmonary <a href="/wiki/Plethysmograph" title="Plethysmograph">plethysmographs</a> are used to measure <a href="/wiki/Functional_residual_capacity" title="Functional residual capacity">functional residual capacity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Functional residual capacity cannot be measured by tests that rely on breathing out, as a person is only able to breathe a maximum of 80% of their total functional capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applegate2014_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Applegate2014-69">[69]</a></sup> The total lung capacity depends on the person\'s age, height, weight, and sex, and normally ranges between 4 and 6 litres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong2012_34_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong2012_34-67">[67]</a></sup> Females tend to have a 20&#8211;25% lower capacity than males. Tall people tend to have a larger total lung capacity than shorter people. <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">Smokers</a> have a lower capacity than nonsmokers. Thinner persons tend to have a larger capacity. Lung capacity can be increased by physical training as much as 40% but the effect may be modified by exposure to air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applegate2014_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Applegate2014-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laeremans_et_al._2018_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laeremans_et_al._2018-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other lung function tests include <a href="/wiki/Spirometry" title="Spirometry">spirometry</a>, measuring the amount (volume) and flow of air that can be inhaled and exhaled. The maximum volume of breath that can be exhaled is called the <a href="/wiki/Vital_capacity" title="Vital capacity">vital capacity</a>. In particular, how much a person is able to exhale in one second (called <a href="/wiki/Spirometry#Parameters" title="Spirometry">forced expiratory volume</a> (FEV1)) as a proportion of how much they are able to exhale in total (FEV). This ratio, the FEV1/FEV ratio, is important to distinguish whether a lung disease is <a href="/wiki/Restrictive_lung_disease" title="Restrictive lung disease">restrictive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_lung_disease" title="Obstructive lung disease">obstructive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_48-14"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong2012_34_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong2012_34-67">[67]</a></sup> Another test is that of the lung\'s  <a href="/wiki/Diffusing_capacity" title="Diffusing capacity">diffusing capacity</a> &#8211; this is a measure of the transfer of gas from air to the blood in the lung capillaries.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy#Respiratory_system" title="Bird anatomy">Bird anatomy &#167;&#160;Respiratory system</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BirdRespiration.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3395" data-file-width="2803" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/BirdRespiration.svg/220px-BirdRespiration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/BirdRespiration.svg/330px-BirdRespiration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/BirdRespiration.svg/440px-BirdRespiration.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BirdRespiration.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>On inhalation, air travels to air sacs near the back of a bird. The air then passes through the lungs to air sacs near the front of the bird, from where the air is exhaled.</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cross-current_exchanger.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Cross-current_exchanger.jpg/220px-Cross-current_exchanger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Cross-current_exchanger.jpg/330px-Cross-current_exchanger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Cross-current_exchanger.jpg/440px-Cross-current_exchanger.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cross-current_exchanger.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cross-current respiratory gas exchanger in the lungs of birds. Air is forced from the air sacs unidirectionally (from right to left in the diagram) through the parabronchi. The pulmonary capillaries surround the parabronchi in the manner shown (blood flowing from below the parabronchus to above it in the diagram).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AvResp_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-AvResp-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graham_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-graham-72">[72]</a></sup> Blood or air with a high oxygen content is shown in red; oxygen-poor air or blood is shown in various shades of purple-blue.</div></div></div>\n<p>The lungs of birds are relatively small, but are connected to 8 or 9 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_sacs" title="Air sacs">air sacs</a> that extend through much of the body, and are in turn connected to air spaces within the bones. On inhalation, air travels through the trachea of a bird into the air sacs. Air then travels continuously from the air sacs at the back, through the lungs, which are relatively fixed in size, to the air sacs at the front. From here, the air is exhaled. These fixed size lungs are called "circulatory lungs", as distinct from the "bellows-type lungs" found in most other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AvResp_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-AvResp-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maina2005_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maina2005-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The lungs of birds contain millions of tiny parallel passages called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parabronchi" title="Parabronchi">parabronchi</a>. Small sacs called <i>atria</i> radiate from the walls of the tiny passages; these, like the alveoli in other lungs, are the site of <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> by simple diffusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maina2005_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maina2005-73">[73]</a></sup> The blood flow around the parabronchi and their atria forms a cross-current process of gas exchange (see diagram on the right).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AvResp_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-AvResp-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graham_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-graham-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The air sacs, which hold air, do not contribute much to gas exchange, despite being thin-walled, as they are poorly vascularised. The air sacs expand and contract due to changes in the volume in the thorax and abdomen. This volume change is caused by the movement of the sternum and ribs and this movement is often synchronised with movement of the flight muscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-VB-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Parabronchi in which the air flow is unidirectional are called <i>paleopulmonic parabronchi</i> and are found in all birds. Some birds, however, have, in addition, a lung structure where the air flow in the parabronchi is bidirectional. These are termed <i>neopulmonic parabronchi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maina2005_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Maina2005-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reptiles">Reptiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Reptiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reptile_anatomy#Respiratory_system" title="Reptile anatomy">Reptile anatomy &#167;&#160;Respiratory system</a></div>\n<p>The lungs of most reptiles have a single bronchus running down the centre, from which numerous branches reach out to individual pockets throughout the lungs. These pockets are similar to alveoli in mammals, but much larger and fewer in number. These give the lung a sponge-like texture. In <a href="/wiki/Tuatara" title="Tuatara">tuataras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>, the lungs are simpler in structure, similar to that of typical amphibians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-VB-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Snakes and limbless lizards typically possess only the right lung as a major respiratory organ; the left lung is greatly reduced, or even absent. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphisbaenian" title="Amphisbaenian">Amphisbaenians</a>, however, have the opposite arrangement, with a major left lung, and a reduced or absent right lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-VB-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodylia" title="Crocodylia">crocodilians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monitor_Lizard" title="Monitor Lizard">monitor lizards</a> have developed lungs similar to those of birds, providing an unidirectional airflow and even possessing air sacs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The now extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pterosauria" title="Pterosauria">pterosaurs</a> have seemingly even further refined this type of lung, extending the airsacs into the wing membranes and, in the case of <a href="/wiki/Lonchodectidae" title="Lonchodectidae">lonchodectids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tupuxuara" title="Tupuxuara">tupuxuara</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Azhdarchoidea" title="Azhdarchoidea">azhdarchoids</a>, the hindlimbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">Reptilian</a> lungs typically receive air via expansion and contraction of the ribs driven by <a href="/wiki/Axial_skeleton" title="Axial skeleton">axial muscles</a> and buccal pumping. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodilian" title="Crocodilian">Crocodilians</a> also rely on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatic" title="Hepatic">hepatic</a> piston method, in which the liver is pulled back by a muscle anchored to the <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubic bone</a> (part of the pelvis) called the diaphragmaticus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munns_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Munns-77">[77]</a></sup> which in turn creates negative pressure in the crocodile\'s thoracic cavity, allowing air to be moved into the lungs by Boyle\'s law. <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">Turtles</a>, which are unable to move their ribs, instead use their forelimbs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pectoral_girdle" title="Pectoral girdle">pectoral girdle</a> to force air in and out of the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-VB-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amphibians">Amphibians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Amphibians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Axolotl_ganz.jpg"><img alt="Axolotl" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="2178" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Axolotl_ganz.jpg/220px-Axolotl_ganz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Axolotl_ganz.jpg/330px-Axolotl_ganz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Axolotl_ganz.jpg/440px-Axolotl_ganz.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Axolotl_ganz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Axolotl" title="Axolotl">axolotl</a> (<i>Ambystoma mexicanum</i>) retains its larval form with gills into adulthood</div></div></div>\n<p>The lungs of most <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> are simple and balloon-like, with gas exchange limited to the outer surface of the lung. This is not very efficient, but amphibians have low metabolic demands and can also quickly dispose of carbon dioxide by diffusion across their skin in water, and supplement their oxygen supply by the same method. Amphibians employ a <a href="/wiki/Positive_pressure" title="Positive pressure">positive pressure</a> system to get air to their lungs, forcing air down into the lungs by <a href="/wiki/Buccal_pumping" title="Buccal pumping">buccal pumping</a>. This is distinct from most higher vertebrates, who use a breathing system driven by <a href="/wiki/Negative_room_pressure" title="Negative room pressure">negative pressure</a> where the lungs are inflated by expanding the rib cage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breathing_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Breathing-78">[78]</a></sup> In buccal pumping, the floor of the mouth is lowered, filling the mouth cavity with air. The throat muscles then presses the throat against the underside of the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a>, forcing the air into the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-review_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-review-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to the possibility of respiration across the skin combined with small size, all known lungless <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a> are amphibians. The majority of salamander species are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungless_salamander" title="Lungless salamander">lungless salamanders</a>, which respirate through their skin and tissues lining their mouth. This necessarily restrict their size: all are small and rather thread-like in appearance, maximising skin surface relative to body volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duellman&amp;Trueb_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duellman&amp;Trueb-80">[80]</a></sup> Other known lungless tetrapods are the <a href="/wiki/Bornean_flat-headed_frog" title="Bornean flat-headed frog">Bornean flat-headed frog</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atretochoana_eiselti" title="Atretochoana eiselti">Atretochoana eiselti</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Caecilian" title="Caecilian">caecilian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The lungs of amphibians typically have a few narrow internal walls (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/septum" title="wikt:septum">septa</a>) of soft tissue around the outer walls, increasing the respiratory surface area and giving the lung a honey-comb appearance. In some salamanders even these are lacking, and the lung has a smooth wall. In caecilians, as in snakes, only the right lung attains any size or development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-VB-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lungfish">Lungfish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Lungfish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The lungs of <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a> are similar to those of amphibians, with few, if any, internal septa. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_lungfish" title="Australian lungfish">Australian lungfish</a>, there is only a single lung, albeit divided into two lobes. Other lungfish and <i><a href="/wiki/Polypterus" title="Polypterus">Polypterus</a></i>, however, have two lungs, which are located in the upper part of the body, with the connecting duct curving around and above the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>. The blood supply also twists around the esophagus, suggesting that the lungs originally evolved in the ventral part of the body, as in other vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_74-5"><a href="#cite_note-VB-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Invertebrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system_of_gastropods" title="Respiratory system of gastropods">Respiratory system of gastropods</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="503" data-file-width="1148" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/240px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/360px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/480px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Book lungs of spider (shown in pink)</div></div></div>\n<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> have lung-like structures that serve a similar respiratory purpose as, but are not evolutionarily related to, vertebrate lungs. Some <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a>, have structures called <a href="/wiki/Book_lung" title="Book lung">book lungs</a> used for atmospheric gas exchange. Some species of spider have four pairs of book lungs but most have two pairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Scorpions have <a href="/wiki/Spiracle" title="Spiracle">spiracles</a> on their body for the entrance of air to the book lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Coconut_crab" title="Coconut crab">coconut crab</a> is terrestrial and uses structures called <a href="/wiki/Branchiostegal_lung" title="Branchiostegal lung">branchiostegal lungs</a> to breathe air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farrelly2005_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farrelly2005-85">[85]</a></sup> They cannot swim and would drown in water, yet they possess a rudimentary set of gills. They can breathe on land and hold their breath underwater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The branchiostegal lungs are seen as a developmental adaptive stage from water-living to enable land-living, or from fish to amphibian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonates" title="Pulmonates">Pulmonates</a> are mostly <a href="/wiki/Land_snail" title="Land snail">land snails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slug" title="Slug">slugs</a> that have developed a simple lung from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mantle_cavity" title="Mantle cavity">mantle cavity</a>. An externally located opening called the <a href="/wiki/Pneumostome" title="Pneumostome">pneumostome</a> allows air to be taken into the mantle cavity lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_origins">Evolutionary origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Evolutionary origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The lungs of today\'s terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_bladder" title="Gas bladder">gas bladders</a> of today\'s <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> are believed to have evolved from simple sacs, as outpocketings of the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>, that allowed early fish to gulp air under oxygen-poor conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmer_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-farmer-90">[90]</a></sup> These outpocketings first arose in the  <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">bony fish</a>. In most of the <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">ray-finned fish</a> the sacs evolved into closed off gas bladders, while a number of <a href="/wiki/Carp" title="Carp">carp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">trout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eels</a> have retained the <a href="/wiki/Physostome" title="Physostome">physostome</a> condition with the sack being open to the esophagus. In more basal bony fish, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gar" title="Gar">gar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bichir" title="Bichir">bichir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bowfin" title="Bowfin">bowfin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">lobe-finned fish</a>, the bladders have evolved to primarily function as lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmer_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-farmer-90">[90]</a></sup> The lobe-finned fish gave rise to the land-based <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>. Thus, the lungs of vertebrates are <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> to the gas bladders of fish (but not to their <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Atelectasis" title="Atelectasis">Atelectasis</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">Drowning</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Interstitial_lung_disease" title="Interstitial lung disease">Interstitial lung disease</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liquid_breathing" title="Liquid breathing">Liquid breathing</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lung_abscess" title="Lung abscess">Lung abscess</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lung_microbiome" title="Lung microbiome">Lung microbiome</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">Mechanical ventilation</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pores_of_Kohn" title="Pores of Kohn">Pores of Kohn</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Secarecytosis" title="Secarecytosis">Secarecytosis</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-GRAYS-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-GRAYS_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GRAYS_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GRAYS_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GRAYS_1-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GRAYS_1-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GRAYS_1-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GRAYS_1-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Drake, Richard L.; Vogl, Wayne; Mitchell, Adam W.M. (2014). <i>Gray\'s anatomy for students</i> (3rd ed.). Edinburgh: <a href="/wiki/Churchill_Livingstone" title="Churchill Livingstone">Churchill Livingstone</a>/<a href="/wiki/Elsevier" title="Elsevier">Elsevier</a>. pp.&#160;167&#8211;174. <a
